1/1
Research Question: 
“Which validated anxiety assessment methods have been applied in the pediatric emergency 
setting on children?”
Brainstorm on synonyms and associations:
Anxiety: Fear, Scared, Anxious, +State, Worried, Uneasy, stress
Assess*: Determine, Decide, Measure, Detect
Method*: Tool, procedure, technique, system, process
Pediatric: Clinical, hospital, practice
Child*: Kid*, adolescent, teenager 
Validat*: Effect*
Common topics/words removed from title:
Dentist, Dental, adult, chronic, “anxiety disorder”, long-term, childbirth, birth
Possible exclusion criteria:
Adult test group, Non-english material, prior to 2013, children younger than the age of 4.
DTU Findit Search string, 16-02-2023:
(Anxiety AND state AND (Fear OR Scared OR Anxious OR Worried OR Uneasy OR stress) AND 
(Assess* OR Determine OR Decide OR Measure OR Detect) AND (Method* OR Tool OR scale OR 
Meter) AND (Pediatric OR Clinical OR hospital OR practice OR Emergency) AND (child* OR kid) 
AND (effect* OR validat*) AND NOT (Dentist OR Dental OR adult OR chronic OR "anxiety disorder" 
OR long-term OR childbirth OR birth OR cancer)
PubMed Search string, 16-02-2023:

Effects of Educational Video on
Pre-operative Anxiety in Children - A
Randomized Controlled Trial
Härter et al.
2021
STOA
90
6-17
The STOA has already been validated.  For children and adolescents, psychometric analyses were conducted within
this study (Cronbach’s α, factor analysis), which confirm good reliability and factorial validity (e.g., α = 0.91 for
children’s and parent's state anxiety at t1, manuscript in preparation).
With remark, the STOA is a validated questionnaire used in a previous study only with adults.
The STOA has been demonstrated to be a reliable and factorially valid measurement instrument for the parallel
assessment of both state and trait anxiety among children and their parents.

version of the modified Yale Preoperative

mYPAS
(Turkish)
120
5 - 12
The reliability and validity of the m-YPAS were shown.
Turkish version of the m-YPAS can be used as a reliable and valid tool in future studies. (The interrater reliability,
concurrent validity, sensitivity, specificity, and positive and negative predictive values were analyzed.)
Behavioral Strategies to Minimize
Procedural Distress During In-Office
Pediatric Tympanostomy Tube Placement
Without Sedation or Restraint
Cohen et al.
2021
FLACC
120 /102
(6m-4y)/5-12
Research assistants were trained to use the FLACC coding by practising on prior tube placement study data to ensure
interrater reliability.
In addition, reliability analyses utilizing the calculation of a weighted Cohen’s Kappa statistic were conducted to
determine agreement across all FLACC categories (i.e., faces, legs, activity, cry, consolability). Once coders were in
agreement and Kappa was 0.6 or greater, the next batch of subjects was assigned to the coders.
Pediatric surgery and self-reported
anxiety in children and their parents: A
psychometric analysis of the state-trait
operation anxiety (STOA) questionnaire
Barkmann et al. 2023
STOA
90
6-17
The results support the findings of Krohne et al. with regard to factorial validity and reliability. However, the two-factorial
structure of the STOA-S (affective/cognitive components) was not replicable.
In this study, the internal consistency test yielded a value of Cronbach’s α of .93. Also, construct validity was measured
by Pearson correlation analysis, which showed that the STOA is a reliable questionnaire for assessing surgery-related
anxiety and is consistent with the results of the APAIS and a visual analogue scale.

The six-item short-form of the STAI produces scores similar to those obtained using the full  20-item STAI. It has

STAIC vs
mYPAS
76
2-16
The m-YPAS scale is validated for its use in children between 2 and 12 years of age.
Inter-observer reliability in the assessment of the mYPAS scale proved to be very good, with an intraclass correlation
coefficient of 0.949 at M0 (95% confidence interval CI, 0.895-0.975); 0.800 at M1 (95% CI, 0.607-0.904); and 0.838 at
M2 (95% CI, 0.682-0.922).
STAI: The test re-test has a high correlation (0.73-0.86), and its validity has been demonstrated with high and low
levels of anxiety in studies performed on large cohorts of students. The correlation values obtained of 0.83 to 0.94
suggest a very strong validity.
STAIC: The reliability assessed with the Cronbach alpha coefficient was 0.82 for boys and 0.87 for girls, implying an
acceptable internal consistency of the scale to measure anxiety
This finding also translates into a concurrent validity between the STAIC-T and the specific mYPAS scale administered
at that same time point.

The Yale Preoperative Anxiety Scale -
How Does It Compare with a "Gold
Standard"?
Kain et al.
1997
mYPAS vs
STAIC
51
5-12
Currently, the "gold standard" for anxiety evaluation is Spielberger's State-Trait Anxiety Inventory (STAI).
Concurrent validity was assessed by the correlation between the m-YPAS total score and the STAIC was good (P =
0.01, r = 0.79). Construct validity was assessed by comparing the m-YPAS scores at three increasingly stressful points
(i.e., preoperative holding, induction Phase 1, and induction Phase 2).
mYPAS: This instrument has good to excellent observer reliability, and good validity compared with a gold
standard(STAI).
In conclusion, showing good to excellent observer reliability and good concurrent and construct validity, the m-YPAS
proves to be an excellent tool for assessing children's anxiety during the perioperative period.
mYPAS: This instrument can be used for children aged two to six years, can be completed in less than one minute, and
has good inter- and intraobserver reliability data (kappaW = 0.66-0.91).
Validation of a clinically useful measure of
children's state anxiety before medical
procedures
Ersig et al.
2013
Children's
Anxiety
Meter-State
(CAM-S), VAS,
STAI-C
421
-
VAS is a horizontal 10-point to assess children's state anxiety and performed initial validation with 60 children, 7–13
years old, having tonsillectomies. Scores on the scale were significantly associated (p < .001) with the state scale of
the STAI-C.
This analysis provides evidence of the construct validity of a brief vertical analogue scale, the CAM-S, to assess
self-reported state anxiety in young children.
What are the validity and reliability of the
modified Yale Preoperative Anxiety
Scale-Short Form in children less than 2
years old?
Kühlmann et al. 2018
mYPAS
(Dutch),
COMFORT-B
129
0,9 months to
16,5 months
These findings support the validity and reliability of the Dutch version of the mYPAS short form in children less than 2
years old.
Our results confirm our hypotheses that the mYPAS-SF has sufficient validity and reliability to support the use of this
scale for evaluating preoperative anxiety in children less than 2 years old. The original mYPAS has proven its validity
for over 20 years. It has been translated into other languages and tested with good results in many different
populations. The mYPAS-SF remains the most used scale for assessing preoperative anxiety in children aged 2 years
and above.
Our results show good reliability at induction of anaesthesia, and moderate to good reliability at the holding area.
Previous validation studies have reported lower inter-rater reliability at the holding area as well. The decreased
reliability can in part be explained by the low variance in scores, as 75% of the infants had low scores on the
mYPAS-SF at the holding area.
The kappa for the results of ten paired assessments for the mYPAS-SF was 0.82, reflecting sufficient inter-rater
reliability.
Internal consistency was excellent for mYPAS-SF (Cronbach's alpha 0.93 at the holding area and 0.93 at induction of
anaesthesia). Inter-rater reliability on scale levels was tested over n = 90 observations and showed moderate reliability
at the holding area (ICC (95% CI) = 0.57(0.42-0.70) and good reliability at the induction of anaesthesia (ICC =
0.81(0.71-0.87)).
Development of a Short Version of the
Modified Yale Preoperative Anxiety Scale
Jenkins et al.
2014
mYPAS Short
form
3798
mean=5,63
The mYPAS measure has strong internal reliability, interrater reliability, and convergent validity.
The Cronbach αs for the reduced item set (4 items) during each time point were at least 0.92. All α values were above
0.90. Therefore, the α values of the reduced item set were considered to be sufficient, indicating that the internal
reliability of the measure was not compromised when removing 1 item.
Assessing Children’s State Anxiety
Schisler et al.
1998
STAIC
881
5 - 18
Two other points suggest that there were individual items in the tools that affected reliability and validity. Despite
conscientious efforts to ensure all items were completed, there was a high rate of item omission. Spielberger et al.
advised that if children omit more than two items, their scores on the STAIY and STAIC should be considered invalid.
We found some support for the construct validity of the STAIC.
In summary, the findings of this study do not support the reliability or validity of the STAIY or STAIC for use with children
in hospitals or children undergoing invasive medical procedures.

2 - 7
mYPAS: The scale has previously shown good to excellent inter- and intra-observer reliability and validity.
mYPAS Danish: Face validity - The focus group agreed that the various items captured the essential information of
children’s preoperative anxiety and that the items were recognisable and relevant in everyday clinical practice.
Interrater reliability: The agreements were good to very good at both inductions (Induction 1:kapha;w: 0.63-0.98;
induction 2:kapha;w: 0.72-0.96).
Nilsson et al.
2008
FLACC, CAS
80
5-16
Concurrent validity was supported by the correlation between FLACC scores and the children’s self-reported CAS
scores during the procedure (r=0.59, P<0.05). Construct validity was demonstrated by the increase in median FLACC
score to 1 during the procedure compared with 0 before and after the procedure (P<0.001). Interrater reliability during
the procedure was supported by adequate kappa statistics for all items and for the total FLACC scores (κ=0.85,
P<0.001).
The findings of this study support the use of FLACC as a valid and reliable tool for assessing procedural pain in
children aged 5–16 years.
The FLACC has been found to have good interrater reliability and validity for evaluating pain after surgery, trauma,
malignancy, and other disease processes in infants and children up to 7 years of age.
The results of this study verified concurrent and construct validity of the FLACC scale during procedural pain.
We could also verify construct validity with differences between scores during compared with before and after the
procedure. For postoperative care, the construct validity has only been established in children aged 0–7 years by
measuring pre- and post-analgesia.
Despite the low scores, there were significant differences during the procedure as compared with before and after the
procedure, demonstrating good construct validity.

and validation

Reducing Children’s Anxiety in Hospital Waiting

Stakeholder Map
Appendix title:   
07/02-2023   
Master thesis:
Appendix no.:   
Page no.:   
A.7
1/1
The Patients
The patients that the nurses want us to target are primarily children 
aged 5-8, as these “have developed the skills to understand what 
is going on and the consequences, but lack the coping skills 
regarding anxiety/fear etc.”
Also, “the children may be capable of distinguishing between pain 
and anxiety at this age.”
Triage Patient Observations
1-2-year old blood sample (Room):
Unfortunately ‘Trylleplaster’ (numbing/anaesthetic band-aid) does 
not sit on tiny fingers.
Gitte was nice to the child, who is clearly interested in her. She 
speaks in a smooth voice and gives the child a stick to distract it. 
When the needle touches the finger, the child starts crying. Not 
because it hurts but because Gitte is gripping the hand of the child 
firmly (and she is immobilized). Gitte sings for the child as the latter 
is bawling her eyes out until she lets go of her hand. It takes 
around 2-3 minutes. The child stops crying as Gitte puts on a 
lion-printed band-aid on the finger. She also distracts the child 
with a teddy bear.
4 year old, boy (Room):
The boy is watching Paw Patrol on the screen within the room. He 
is frightened when Gitte enters and probably thinks: “What is she 
going to do to me?”. The presence of a stranger is frightening.
6 year old, boy (Waiting Room):
The boy and his family have been waiting for a while. Gitte wants 
to give the boy a friendly and caring first impression. She asks him 
if he would like to have an ice cream. He is happy and Gitte 
seems to have won his trust.
6 year old, boy - Infected toe (Triage Room):
The boy is in great pain. He is looking at the drawings on the wall 
as the mother and the nurse are examining his toe. 
He is clearly familiar with the triage procedure as he initiates the 
measurements himself.
He does not seem nervous. His pulse is measured and his stom-
ach is looked at, but the pain score is not performed - as they 
know he is in pain due to the infected toe.
Nurse conversation:
Nurse 1: "Would you mind looking at this toe?"
Nurse 2: "Ouch.."
The boy is transferred to a room for the doctor to see him.
4 year old, boy - Screaming (Triage Room):
The boy does not want to be examined at all. He is scared and screaming. He is 
clearly tired and sick (fever). The nurse cannot get close to him, and she calls for 
assistance. A male doctor enters the room. He is very calm and speaks in a calm 
manner: “Hvor er du god, det er så flot” (Translates: “Great, you are doing 
good”).
The doctor clearly has the touch as he kneels before the boy. He meets the boy 
at eye level (or lower) - the child is still screaming, however. The doctor is 
unfazed. 
With help from the parents, the doctor examines his pulse (stethoscope). After 
the examination the boy is calm. While speaking to the parents the doctor is still 
examining the boy's behaviour and tells them that they can go home (as the 
child is okay).
The patient moved up on the waiting list and probably skipped 3 hours of waiting 
time, but it is a trade-off which the doctor chose, as the child was so difficult to 
examine.
15 years old, Girl - Experiencing ‘Epileptic seizures’:
-
The nurse called out her name in the waiting room.
-
She introduced herself to the patient and her parents as we walked to the 
triage room.
-
The girl was visibly nervous (her fingers were shaking and she seemed to be 
on the verge of tears.)
-
The nurse asked her whether she had tried the [PEWS] measurements 
before. The girl answered calmly that she had.
-
Her pulse measurement resulted in the Yellow (medium/high) triage level. She 
did not have a fever (37 Celsius)
-
The nurse was documenting her every word in the computer system but was 
also very aware of the girl and giving her attention. She also told her not to be 
nervous and gently touched her leg.
-
The girl was then transferred to a hospital room, where she would wait for 
another nurse to show up before the doctor would arrive. Only one nurse is 
assigned to the triage and therefore she will not examine her further.
-
The girl could use the iPad in the room for streaming services such as Netflix.

First mYPAS test at HH waiting area
Appendix title:   
13/02-2023   
Master thesis:
Appendix no.:   
Page no.:   
A.10
1/1
Experienced implications of the scoring system:
- The scoring system is extensive to remember the first couple of times (learning curve).
- Understanding the body language of the child is a skill, which will differ among individuals using the scoring system.
- The mYPAS score is a mere snapshot in time, documenting the current anxiety state of the child. It was experienced that this 
could quickly change, as seen with child 2.  When parents gave the child attention, the latter seeked to be comforted, hence the 
“use of parent” score was escalated. Also, when the child talked about the fish, he seemed relaxed, but when talking about his 
reason for being at the hospital, he was more tense. Furthermore, when the nurse comes to examine the child, the score spikes.
- The method is difficult to use on people passing by, as their vocalisations are inaudible and the different criteria is formulated 
towards people sitting still.
mYPAS documentation
Initial mYPAS test

Interview with the Design Team of M.E. Hospital
Appendix title:   
07/03-2023   
Master thesis:
Appendix no.:   
Page no.:   
A.11
1/1
Interview with design team of the new Mary Elisabeths Hospital
Mary Elisabeths Hospital is the new hospital build for children and pregnant 
women in Copenhagen. It is build ajoint to Rigshospitalet and is set to be finished 
in 2025. While it is being build a design team are to design the interior set 
in their office beside the construction site. We had an interview with two 
members of the design team Capser Mathiesen and Esben Dyrholm Hansen, 
about their approach to designing for children and creating a safe and homely 
environment for the children.
Testing
The design team have build a 1:1 of an patient room to test the design.
They invite families to come test their environment (rooms) - they quickly discov-
er pain points when they film how the users interact with the setting.
Pain points are how they will document and measure performance. If no one 
questions a thing or voices their concerns the artefact/design is a success.
Usability and user experience is difficult to measure, as it is such a subjective 
and complex topic. Too many parameters and differences between tests, hence 
it is difficult to capture meaningful values. Therefore, testing is often qualitative - 
and will be documented by the testing team, who will follow up with doctors, 
nurses and patients to track performance. 
Testhuset (The Test House)
Home
Hospital
Preparation
User journey
A cycle between home and the hospital due to the patients often 
satying long term and coming back again. To better the treatment 
the design team wants to focus on preparing the users for their 
hospitlisation. Providing information before hand for both the child 
and parents/guadians as well as getting to know the patient and 
their prefences for their next visit.
Room design 
The rooms and hospital are meant for children. But children are not their only user. 
In fact they are the minority when considering parents, family, friends and hospital 
personnel. The design should aim to be welcoming and feel like home rather than 
being specifically catered to Peppa pig enjoyers. 
"Who have Peppa pig painted on their wall at home?"
By making the experience feel as though you are at home (the normal day), the 
parents may relax - and so might the child because of that.
Knowing the users (children)
At riget they will know that a child, William, likes football, they will 
introduce him to the medical treatment in a fun way and memorable 
one, that they can use on him in the future.
The next time William visits, the doctor will have a note: "William likes 
Manchester United, you should show him the medical examination 
on a teddy bear, he liked that. And don't forget to give him an 
ice-cream with chocolate taste".
In contrast Herlev does not know anything about their patients 
coming in. They know the name, the age and the reason for being 
there. They do not know if they are scared of needles or if they are 
known in the psychiatry (have anxiety disorder and might be very 
nervous).
Focus for M.E. 
hospital
A VR- website to experience the design of the patient 
rooms
https://api2.enscape3d.com/v3/view/49a820-
fa-5d19-41eb-983f-5ebafceba05c
Theme: Nature to the body. Going 
from the outer circle of nature to the 
inner circle of the body and examina-
tions.
Family
Make the experience better for the entire family = better for the child. 
Create playing areas for siblings and working spaces for the parents. 
Create an everyday routine for the family. Give them tasks to perform 
like cleaning up after themselves, cooking and washing their own 
clothes. Give them a choice! Maybe it is more troublesome, but it gives 
them choice and meaning.
“We design for the hospital to be homely, not just cater to the child. It 
should not be a childish design like Winnie the Pooh, due to the awake 
children are only approximately 8% of the users. The rest is the staff 
and the parents, patient of older age.”

XRk

Reducing Children’s Anxiety in Hospital Waiting 
Areas through a User Centric System Design.
Interview with Stephanie, Hospital Clown at HH
Appendix title:   
09/03-2023   
Master thesis:
Appendix no.:   
Page no.:   
A.12
1/1
Hospital clown interview
About Stephanie
She has been a hospital clown for 10 years, and have been working 
at Herlev for 7-8 years. She works full time with 3 days a week in the 
hospital (shifts of appr. 3-4 hour) and 2 days in the office doing 
administratively work such as talks and social media.
When working at the hospital she is Split, who she does not see as a 
character, but more as an energy she brings. She is not playing 
someone else, which she sees as an restriction while communicating 
with the children. She is herself, which also make for a more “true” 
connection with the children.
Education 
It is a 1 year long education to become a hospital clown. Mainly it is 
based on a master apprentice program, where you follow a master 
out on shifts. It is important to learn to “fail” (get rejected) and to 
understand the children and there needs. A lot of the education is 
also learning impovisation, being present and adaptation readiness.
It is also encouraged to learn different skills like juggeling, playing 
music, ballons etc. Danske Hospitalsklovne funds buying different 
objects like bubbles, instruments, teddys etc. for the clowns to use 
while working.
When working a shift you will be in pairs. This is an advantage: 
-
If one person is sick, there will still be a clown present that day.
-
Two people to read and understand the children’s needs.
-
A collegue to discuss experiences with.
-
Working together creating perfomances for children who might 
be more comfortable watching interact, than being part of the 
interaction.
 
https://danskehospitalsklovne.dk/node/27
Goal
The goal for the hospital clown is not making the anxiety disap-
pear. It is to match and respect the energy/mood of the child. 
Perhaps through the interaction the mood can change, but it is 
not the main goal. It is to reassure the child: “ It is okay to be 
feeling what you are feeling. It is okay with me.” Whether that is 
laughter or crying.
Another function the clowns preform is the opportunity to 
decline an invitation. In an environment where everything is 
controlled by someone else (when you can see a doctor, what 
examnitations you need, when you can leave etc.) it is nice to 
have some control over a situation. To be seen and given an 
invitation to play, talk, be distracted etc., but also have the 
opportunity to decline.
Reading the children
It is very subjective how the clowns read the mood/ener-
gy of the children. Mostly they look at body language, 
eye contact and experience.
This is also how they determine how long they need to 
stay with the child. This could be 5 minutes or 1,5 hours, 
which is important while working: There is no time 
schedule. It is a flow being present and trying to interact 
with as many children as possible .
Key take aways
-
Timing: Meet with the child as early in their stay as possible that will 
have the greatest effect. The clowns can’t do much if the child is 
already anxious, it has to be caught while building up to make a 
difference. E.g. in the waiting area or just before the blood sample.
-
Presence: Having the clowns present at the hospital make a great 
difference. Even if patients do not interact with them, watching their 
interaction and impact with others have an effect on the general 
atmosphere. 
-
Invitation: By giving all the patient an invitation to interact makes 
them feel seen, and gives them control to decline the invitation 
giving them control over something in an environment, where they 
do not have any.
-
Parents: If the parents are nervous/anxious it is as much a interac-
tion with them as the children, as they mirror each others emotinal 
state.
The Parents
Many children need their parents to be present while 
they interact with the clown. And some asks their parent 
implicitly if it is okay to talk/play with the clown.
The children often mirror their parents emotional state. If 
the parents are nervous so are the children. Therefore, it 
is not only an interaction to help the children, but also 
the parents, if they are very nervous/anxious.
Enviroment
Stephanie highlighted Slagelse Ambulatorie as having a 
good waiting area. The area has been designed by an 
artist. This makes a difference in the patients behavior 
while waiting. They use the room more: Exploring, look-
ing, interacting with their environment.

Interview with Carsten - Tools
Teacher, Therapist and (co)-creator of FACE:IT 
FACE:IT Bo is a magnet board that allows children and the practitioner to build 
faces in order to visualise and externalise feelings - this helps facilitate dialogue on 
this difficult topic. The face can be hung on a wall, and easily introduced in discus-
sions when needed. It can be used in a playfu l way, that extends to more tha n 
just the faces - teddy bear, blowing bubbles etc. 
   From FACE:IT - Bo can (be):
- Used for emotional mirroring.
- Used to express feelings.
- Initiate a change of feelings.
- Help create awareness about and experiences with the changeability of emo-
tions.
- Used in conversations and work with individual children and in group contexts.
- Used to express what is sometimes difficult to say in words.
- Help children become better at reading others.
- Help to externalize feelings that can be difficult to contain.
Humørmåtten is a tool that tries to personify 5 feelings by 
introducing movement.  The format is an 80 cm wide 
mousepad that can be put on the floor/table
The 5 feelings correspond to those of the Disney movie 
“Inside Out”.  
(From left: Sadness, Disgust, Joy, Fear, Anger). 
The scale (0-10) is used to point out the intensity of the 
feeling. 
Humørsmåleren is a wall 
poster, that can help explore 
different feelings and their 
intensities. 
They are also references for 
the Bo face, which can quickly 
be created. The intensity 
discussed is as humørmåtten 
from 0-10. 
Different feelings come in 
multiple colours, this is 
purpously done to not intro-
duce a bias among the 
children. For instance, “red 
does not necessarily have to 
be anger, because that could 
also be love or the fact that 
the father of the child supports 
the football club Liverpool, 
who happen to play in red 
jerseys”.
Also, the scale is not linked to 
the placement of the different 
faces, hence “bekymret” 
(transl. worried) does not have 
to be ranked as [0-2], nor 
does that feeling necessarily 
Generally:
The three tools are therapeutic tools for 
dialogue with children. It can be used in 
many settings, if time and skills allow.
Bo
Humørmåleren
Humørmåtten
==PAGE:140==
Reducing Children’s Anxiety in Hospital Waiting 
Areas through a User Centric System Design.
FACE:IT interview - Tools and Insights
Appendix title:   
20/02-2023   
Master thesis:
Appendix no.:   
Page no.:   
A.13
2/2
“When speaking with children about feelings, it is not preferred to ”decide/control” what 
they are feeling.: 
Instead Carsten suggests feelings that the child may feel, but thereby helping the child 
find out what they are feeling:
The tools are visual and physical which can help externalise the feelings and make them 
seem tangible. This is also applied by the Wong-Baker faces. Those faces do not use 
colour.
The tool is then used to give the child exercises to make: “Bo needs to breeth and blow 
bubbles” (CBT to calm child down)
It is okay to feel sad, but it becomes a problem when all you can think of is with the “sad-fil-
ter” . Children often get stuck in their current feeling and might think, that the feeling will last 
forever. Its is therefore important to break this spiral, and focus on other feelings that are 
also present in the moment or have been in the near past:
It may be important to remind the child (patient) that the hospital experience will end. They 
might get an icecream afterwards, they will feel calm again.
When the sad or anxious filter (goggles) are on, you might ignore the otherwise friendly 
experiences around you - such as: The friendly nurse, the delicious softdrink or even the 
comfort of the parent.
When the child is playing, the arousal gradually decreases. “You are rarely alert while enjoy-
ing playing a game”. - Hence, playing a game works as a distraction.
Calming the parent(s) has a direct effect on the child. The child is subconsciously mirroring 
the behavior of the parent. 
Anxiety is an overused concept. Perhaps worry is more fitting for everyday description of the 
emotional state of the children. It is important not to diagnose the children, it will be confus-
ing for them.
Young children do not have the same experiences or vocabulary as adults. As seen in the 
STAIC inventory, many of the words in the self-reported questions are not completely under-
stood and therefore omitted by the children. (Hence skewing the validity of the tool)
If the child only focuses on the bad feeling there will be no space for the good ones. It is impor-
tant to realise that there are a nuance of feelings. There are more than just the anxious ones.
The tool [Bo] is not magic, it does not produce clear results and can be difficult to measure. It 
is more about the process (dialogue with the children) than the actual outcome - reduction in 
anxiety.
Rutines make things seem safe. Therefore FACE:IT are making video material and books to 
help make stressful experiences (like blood samples) become less unknown. “If the child no the 
contents of the story, and that it has a happy ending - it is less dangerous”.
If no other senses are activated it is easy to only experience the anxiety. Perhaps Herlev Hospi-
tal could invoke pleasant experiences through other senses being activated:
Nice smells (vanilla), paintings on the walls, relaxing music, something to fiddle with.
This might be a limitation for the Visual Analogue Scale (VAS): “If you only ask about anxious-
ness or pain, it is the only answer you will get. This might result in an overestimation. 
Practitioner: “How anxious do you feel?”
Child [is crying]: ”10!”
Practitioner: “Is this the worst you have ever felt?”
Child: “No”
Practitioner: “Compared to that time [where you felt the worst] how bad is it?”
Child: “5”
Practitioner: “Okay, do you feel any other feelings, what are you happy about right now?”
>> Other feelings might appear, and one moment later, the pain is perhaps less significant than 
experienced at first.
“You are sad”
“It looks like, that you could be sad.. or maybe.. [open suggestion]”
“What feeling does the presence of your parent give you? Joy?”
Practitioner: “Bo might be anxious, and can therefore feel his hands are shaking and his stomach twirls. 
 
             Why do you think Bo feels like this”
Child: “Because Bo needs to go to the doctor and have a blood sample”
“What did you eat for breakfast? Chokolate cereal? How did that make you feel? Happy?”

Observations at Herlev
Appendix title:   
07/06-2023   
Master thesis:
Appendix no.:   
Page no.:   
A.14
1/2
Obervations at Herlev
-
V: ‘Vurdering’ (translate: assessment) - is assigned by 1813 to children aged 
0-17 years old. These will undergo full triage
-
‘Grey square’: Is assigned by 1813 and is often less of an emergency at first 
glance. It is mainly assigned to children aged 0-11 years, who will most often 
leave the hospital once assessed. The nurse trainee handles these cases as only 
PEWS is measured and not a full triage is required (see triage). The cause is 
primarily ENT (ear-nose-throat) related.
-
S: 'Skade' (translate: damage). This is labelled as triage lvl 5 (blue) and is 
assigned to physical damages to children aged 0-2 years. The children's depart-
ment also handles these as they are experts in being pedagogical. For this track 
they only do the necessary pre-assessment and give painkillers before the 
children are transferred to the adults emergency section.
-
I: 'Indlægges' (translate: hospitalisation). This means that the child will have to 
stay for further observations. The assessment is done by the doctor and the label 
is escalated to I from either V or Grey square.
“Spor”: is assigned by the patient's personal doctor or the 
emergency hotline 1813:
Insights from Herlev Hospital PED
Done through visits where observations, interviews and 
shadowing were used to obtaine the insights 
Shifts (Nurse):
7-15, 15-23, 23-7 (7 am - 3 pm, 3 pm - 11 pm, 
11 pm - 7 am)
(Status) Meeting at the office (4 pm)
The nurses meet to quickly brief each other on the status of patients and divide them among the availa-
ble nurses for the shift. 
They state the age, gender, cause and tests performed: “I have just taken a blood sample”.
Social aspects:
Balloon men, clowns (/ funny personalities) are present for some hours every Tuesday or Wednes-
day.
Waiting time:
Gitte (Nurse): “The waiting time system is unfortunately bad for the patients, there 
is no indicator of when it is their turn. They will have to wait for the personnel, 
which is already very busy.”
“It would be nice to have an anxiety level as a parameter for our overview. Know-
ing when patients are ready for us rather than us for them would be interesting.”
Opportunity:
Having the same nurse (from the triage) look after the patient. 
Pros: The patient feels familiar to that nurse (perhaps reducing anxiety)
Cons: Logistically difficult due to the flow of patients. With two nurses looking at the same patient a 
‘broader’ examination is ensured.
Anxiety among patients - Consequences:
Nurse: “Anxiety among patients should be avoided due to three things”
The patient will have a bad experience (and memory of it)
The time it takes to treat a person increases.
The diagnosis of the patient can be inaccurate - which can result in wrong use of medication / treat-
ment.
Tools to combat anxiety:
To calm down anxious children the nurses can either use toys and or calm words and mannerism. 
However, in some cases force or clinical laughing gas can be used. Also, it has happened that they 
cannot complete the treatment due to reactions of patients.
Triage Scoring
==PAGE:143==
Reducing Children’s Anxiety in Hospital Waiting 
Areas through a User Centric System Design.
Observations at Herlev
Appendix title:   
07/06-2023   
Master thesis:
Appendix no.:   
Page no.:   
A.14
2/2
IT systems the nurses use:
They use sundhedsplatformen which is attached to the system called Epic. It shows them the data 
from the screens in the room (arrivals, spor, treatments, patient info etc.). It can also show the patient's 
medical record, from every visit within “sundhedsplatformen”. This includes psychiatrists, but some-
times the data is locked. Also if the data is available it is not always explicit the reason for the visit or 
the outcome. For instance they do not know if the patient is scared of needles or has a general anxiety 
disorder. Therefore, the nurses rarely know anything about their patient pre-examination. (This is in 
contrast to the very patient centric way of the Mary Elizabeth's hospital)
-
Zebra (Portable display)
The zebra device is a portable phone-sized tablet, which the nurses use to document different 
data of the patients. This way the data is very mobile. For instance, Gitte entered data on the 
patient’s weight and height while moving between rooms. 
Retrospective assessment of the Patient’s experience:
There is currently no retrospective assessment of the patients for further hospital visits. They [nurses] 
document how the patients respond to the treatment, but not the “meta-treatment” (the experience). 
For instance, anxious behaviour because of the nurse, the hospital, needles etc. 
Obervations at Herlev
Insights from Herlev Hospital PED
Done through visits where observations, interviews and 
shadowing were used to obtaine the insights 
Observation:
The nurses are often knowledge-sharing within the office and in the hallway. They help 
each other do different tests even if the responsibility of the patient is not theirs.
“Can anyone do a urine sample?”, “Is number 43 ready to go home?” > “No, he still 
needs a blood sample”
Timing - the waiting area:
A parent to a patient visits the secretary to ask if they have time to go to the hospi-
tal's 7/11 shop (3-min walk from the emergency department). Do they have to wait 
10 minutes or 2 hours, he asks. Gitte says that they can.
But: How do they find them again, when it is their turn? This is an example of “the 
patient is not ready for us”.

==PAGE:150==
Reducing Children’s Anxiety in Hospital Waiting 
Areas through a User Centric System Design.
Conﬂicts and Paradoxes
Appendix title:   
21/02-2023   
Master thesis:
Appendix no.:   
Page no.:   
A.17
1/1
Paradoxes & Controversies
Children wants to feel better, but are resisting treat-
ment, because they are scared of it.
Anxious parents want to help the children feel calm, but 
the more they interact the more their feelings are mir-
rored by the children, hence making the child more 
anxious. 
It is difficult for children to feel at home and relaxed at 
an unfamiliar place. The hospital has a very clinical and 
silent atmosphere that does not invoke these calm 
feelings.
Medical staff wants to give you all the time in the world 
to have the best experience, but they do not have 
enough time.
How can we mitigate this equation of 
clashes leading to anxiety?
Vision: Reduce the “green” anxiety 
(don’t increase!)
Paradox: Two conflicting statements that seemingly 
can’t be true at once.  
Controversy: Conflicting interests of two “worlds”.
Health Sector
Care Taking
Anxiety

==PAGE:154==
Reducing Children’s Anxiety in Hospital Waiting 
Areas through a User Centric System Design.
Time and Flow
Appendix title:   
24/03-2023   
Master thesis:
Appendix no.:   
Page no.:   
A.19
1/1
Time and Flow
Key points and discussion:
Measurements: It makes most sense to measure 3-4 times, and those being after the check in at the secretary 
desk. One measure for each "stage" (if possible). At the coloured areas is where self-report and observation will 
be assessed. Biometrics are captured continiously in this period. It could be beneficial to record and show live 
anxiety data.
Solution accessibility and anxiety reduction: We see the potential in having a solution that is highly accessible, 
meaning that many can access it and see the benefits of it for a long duration. This includes reducing anxiety as 
the solution will be remembered and preferably interacted with outside the waiting area. The earlier the patient is 
greeted by the solution the better.
Not feeling at home: in an unfamiliar  enviroment is natural. In order to truly relax we need to make the hospital 
stay feel more "homely" (familiar).
Flat ambiense: Likewise, as the enviroment is unfamiliar it is also very clinical, and does not invite the patients to 
interact with each other or the facilities. Therefore, waiting is just waiting.
Parental presence: Is unavoidable and can influence how anxious the child is, or even make them more or less 
expressive, hence hiding their true emotions. Parental presence can have a possitive and negative influence on 
the child depending on the level of anxiety of the parent. The solution could also treat parents - as they indeed 
are important stakeholders.
Empathy of nurses: The children do not know the intentions of the nurses, it is only felt in the triage room or the 
examination room. The nurses can be associated with the scary examination but at the same time they can be 
very comforting, with their toys and mannerisms.
Consequences of anxiety: These are mainly felt by the personel when treating the patients (including triage). The 
earlier a good experience is had the less anxious the patient might feel later - starting at the triage room.
Patient influence on each other: occurs when patients are together such as the main waiting area. It can also 
be in the triage room. Emotional mirroring is therefore a possibility in these situations.
Different needs: As patients are different both in treatment and personality, a solution should have the possibility 
to encompas multiple individuals.
Childrens fear: Children are generally scared in this new environment. When focusing on the pain or treatment it 
is even worse. Similarly, fear seems to be at its lowest, when the child is comfortable and distracted - such as in 
the waiting room when watching the fish. For the children to change their behaviour and become less anxious it 
needs for them to participate in this change. The solution therefore may be an invitation rather than another 
controling factor.
Limitations in care: As the staff are generally busy, care is not the main proirity. Therefore a solution could help 
alleviate anxiety by introducing or amplifying existing care.
Limitations in measurements (staff): As the nurses do not go to the waiting area, they have no control in this 
area. It is also difficult for the nurses to examine pain and anxiety, as these are often linked.
Limitations in measurements (us): We may be prohibited access to measure at certain times, for instance in 
examination room. Also, if we measure multiple reportings they might bias/skew each other. Our mere presence 
may also skew the data.
Our inﬂuence

Appendix title:

The Final Concept
Appendix title:   
14/06-2023   
Master thesis:
Appendix no.:   
Page no.:   
A.28
1/1
The Final Concept
The final concept is a figma build prototype that aims 
to reduce children’s anxiety in a PED setting through 
gamification of CBT-exercises with physical arte-
facts. 
The game takes place at Herlev Hopsital PED and in the fictive home land of 
Bjørn the bear doctor introducing the exercises. Along side Emma, a fellow 
patient, the player travels to the homeland of Bjørn to learn different exercis-
es to do if the player “...feel nervous and want to calm down, while finding 
their inner strength”, as Bjørn describes it in the intro. As a reward for doing 
the exercises the player receives breakfast items, such as berries, pancakes 
and juice, to bring back to Bjørn. Through their journey the player meets 
different animal friends of Bjørn: A mother parrot, Danny the dragon and 
Sophia the turtle, while travelling with Emma in an air balloon.
The muscle relaxation the exercise squeeze the 
orange was chosen, as it is a well known
method that can help guide children to relax their 
bodies
The exercise was simplified to only consist of tension 
of the lower arms and hands. To physicalise the 
muscle relaxation exercise an orange looking artefact 
was needed. Since a squishy ball has a high affor-
dance and is a common kids toy, the choice fell on 
that.
For the breathing exercises we aimed to create a physical 
feedback of the players breathing. Therefore the pinwheel 
was chosen through our toy store expedition and rapid 
prototyping. As mentioned, the pinwheel is a common arte-
fact used to help kids practice their deep breathing. In order 
to visually connect the pinwheel to the gameplay, the cloud 
guide was used as a stable base for the pinwheel. 
Bounderry object for the nurses/doc-
tors to talk with the children about to 
distract them during e.g. an examina-
tion. The staff does not need to be up 
to date with the newest child TV.
Tools to use in the waiting area to 
handle the anxious feelings, but also 
for future use outside the hospital. 
Exercises the nurses/doctors can 
bring up e.g. during an examniation to 
relax the player, while not having to 
teach the child something new. “Re-
member when you squeezed juice for 
Bjørn, can you do that again?”
Will change the ambience in the 
waiting room, if there are children 
playing and laughing. Possibly affect-
ing other patients in a positive way.
An activity to do with parents and 
child together, while waiting.
Narration through the game 
guiding the player with the 
gameplay and exercises.
Animation in the game show-
ing when to use the physical 
artefact for the exercises.

Initial Testing of Empatica watch - E4
Appendix title:   
14/06-2023   
Master thesis:
Appendix no.:   
Page no.:   
A.31
1/2
The purpose of this experiment it was to test hypothesis that there is a good agreement between the Empatica 
E4’s HR measures of anxiety, self-reported anxiety and an observers estimation on the TP’s experienced anxiety
Set-up: Watching (https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=Z_-Au4dWuQ0). TP, female aged 25, watched a video 
clip from the horror computer game ”Slenderman”, while wearing the Empatica E4 collecting HR data. An 
observer observed the TP watching the clip noting down every time they observed any signs of the TP being 
scared/anxious. These signs were either categorised as high or low anxiety. After the video ended the TP self-re-
ported which time frames they had felt the most anxious.
Results (data): Downloaded HR-data from the E4, Obervers notations of high/low anxiety, video of TP watching 
the clip and self-reported frames of anxiety
Slenderman: The Eight Pages, is a videogame played in a first person perspective. The player has to move 
through a dark forrest with only flashlight collecting 8 hidden pages while avoiding the Slenderman. The more 
pages you gather the darker and thicker the forrest get, while the Slenderman moves closer to you. The video 
watched was a game play collecting all the 8 pages. (7:47 min)
Initial Testing of Empatica watch - E4
Multiple Measurements
It can be seen that the three measures do not always seem to 
correlate or appear at the same time. At time point 1 the self-re-
port and HR-data indicates a peak, while the observer records a 
slight flinch a bit delayed, when the HR is starting to decrease. 
Between point 1 and 2, the observer marks signs of low anxiety, 
whereas the HR has a small peak, while the self-reporting occurs 
later. In point 2 the three measures all agree that the TP might be 
anxious. This is also the first time, that the TP covers her face in 
horror. Within the video clip, the audio was becoming increasingly 
more notable, as discussed after the test. The music has likely 
helped build anticipation resulting in the increase in HR. Between 
point 2 and 3 the HR is generally decreasing but with a sudden 
spike halfway through. No self-reported anxiety was registered 
but interestingly the observer documented two instances of high 
anxiety, as the TP ”jumped” in the chair.
As for the 3rd and last self-reported timestamp, the HR was at its 
lowest out of the three, while the observer registered the biggest 
visible freight in the form of a big jump.
To conclude the three measures did have some agreements 
and the HR did change a lot over the course of the 7-8 min 
test ranging from 72 to 108 BPM. Therefore, to get a better 
picture of anxiety having multiple measurements makes the
assessment more nuanced.
Conclusion
==PAGE:192==
Reducing Children’s Anxiety in Hospital Waiting 
Areas through a User Centric System Design.
Initial Testing of Empatica watch - E4
Appendix title:   
14/06-2023   
Master thesis:
Appendix no.:   
Page no.:   
A.31
2/2
Purpose:  People have different HR base-lines. Additionally, what scares us is very subjective and so is how we 
react to being scared. This means that bio metric data varies as well, which is what the following test
intended to show. 
Set-up: HR was collected from five TPs, while watching a video clip from the horror computer game Five Nights 
at Freddy’s (https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=Y5e-NJEPfA8&ab_channel=PopyoGames) with headphones on. 
The TPs were 4 Males and 1 Female with ages ranging from 25-28 years.
Results (data): Downloaded HR-data from the E4 from 5 people
Five Nights at Freddy’s: Is another video game, where playing in first person perspective you have to ‘survive’ 5 
nights as a nightshift guard at a puppet museum/circus. The puppets come alive during the night, and you have 
to keep watch through security cameras to make sure they do not come into your office. It is not possible to 
keep both doors closed all the time, as they use too much power.
Five Nights at Freddy’s: https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=Y5e-NJEPfA8&ab_channel=PopyoGames
Initial Testing of Empatica watch - E4
Baselines
As seen from the graphs the TPs had very different HR at the 
beginning of the video (at zero seconds). After the first 100 sec-
onds the TPs seemed to have settled in to watching the video. 
Especially TP3 who seemed to be extremely excited initially had 
dropped to their lowest recorded HR of 67 BPM. From the 100 
second mark the general trend seemed to be a steady increase 
in HR among the five TPs as the 8 minute video clip played out. 
Determining which person was the most anxious is diﬀicult due 
to their different baselines. What we can say however, is that the 
mean HR of the TPs was gradually increasing, thus indicating 
that they were on average more anxious at timepoint 4 than 3 
etc.
Therefore looking at the mean HR at different timepoints 
may be a feasible and accurate measurement
Conclusion
TP1: 25,F
TP2: 26,M
TP3: 26,M
TP4: 28,M
TP5: 27,M

Time point 2
Time point 3
Time point 4

A.34 - Test Protocol
14.06.23
A.34 - Test Protocol
Intervention(s):
An iPad game that may include physical artifacts to help the patient calm down by using
3 cognitive behavioral therapy techniques:
1: Distraction.
-
This is done through the child friendly gameplay and graphics of the game.
2: Deep Breathing.
-
A breathing exercise (where a physical pinwheel is spun by blowing on it
presented by the in-game graphics).
3: Muscle Relaxation.
-
A squeezing exercise (where a squishy ball resembling an orange is used to
“squeeze orange juice” in the game).
Pictures from non physical game intervention.
Control Group:
The patient receives no intervention, but the same measurements are captured.
Measurements: (see table below)
Nurse participation: Text written in blue are measurements made by the nurses.

Time ﬂow - How the students will test - V2.
1.
Contact the child and parent in the waiting room after they have arrived at the
secretary's desk.
2.
Hand out consent form to parent(s).
[Patient Enters Triage]
3.
Measure: Documentation of WBF Anxiety and WBF Pain scores (Xwac1, Xwan1) and
the triage nurse’s proxy rating of child’s WBF anxiety (Xwpc1).
[Patient Leaves Triage]
4.
Document the triage scores - Xwac1, Xwan1 and Xwpc1.
5.
Apply the Empatica E4 watch to the patient's wrist.
6.
Wait 2-3 minutes for the patient to acclimatize.
7.
Measure:
a.
Note down timestamp.
b. Document mYPAS score (Xm2).
c.
After 2 minutes: Note down timestamp. (Xhr2) Completed.
d. Go to the patient and capture self-report WBF anxiety (Xwac2).
8.
Give the patient the intervention (if not enrolled in the control group).
9.
Note down the timestamp (when the game starts - if intervention group).
10. Intervention or waiting time of 10-15 minutes is completed by the patient.
11. Note down the time stamp. (Xhr3) Completed.
